10.1016/j.jmp.2016.07.008
Knowledge structures provide a highly flexible set-theoretic framework for representing the organization of knowledge elements in a domain. Although they were first introduced by  Doignon and Falmagne (1985)  within a purely deterministic approach, this perspective was broadened later on by developing a probabilistic framework on top of them ( Falmagne & Doignon, 1988a,b ). The so-called  basic local independence model  (BLIM;   Doignon & Falmagne, 1999 ) forms the standard probabilistic model within the theory of knowledge structures. Applications of the BLIM raise several questions. How to determine the underlying knowledge structure, and given that, what are the parameter values of the probabilistic model? It is the second question that the current paper addresses. The focus, however, is not on suggesting a particular method for properly estimating the parameters, but to tackle the more fundamental problem of determining whether the estimates obtained with any such method are unique. This property is known as the identifiability of a parametric model. Any such model is considered identifiable when there are no two different sets of parameter values that lead to the same model prediction.

Bamber and van Santen (1985, 2000)  formalize these considerations and provide general results concerning the identifiability of a  parametric model , which they define as a triple  
                      
                         〈 
                         Θ 
                         , 
                         f 
                         , 
                         Φ 
                         〉 
                      
                   . Here the set  
                      Θ 
                      ⊆ 
                      
                         
                            R 
                         
                         
                            n 
                         
                      
                    is considered to form the  parameter space , the set  
                      Φ 
                      ⊆ 
                      
                         
                            R 
                         
                         
                            m 
                         
                      
                    is called the  outcome space , and the  prediction function  
                   
                      f 
                      : 
                      Θ 
                      → 
                      Φ 
                    is a mapping from the parameter space into the outcome space. A model  
                      
                         〈 
                         Θ 
                         , 
                         f 
                         , 
                         Φ 
                         〉 
                      
                    then is said to be  (globally) identifiable  if its prediction function  
                      f 
                    is one-to-one (i.e., injective). It is  locally identifiable  at a point  
                      
                         
                            θ 
                         
                         
                            0

                      Θ 
                    if there is an open neighborhood of  
                      
                         
                            θ 
                         
                         
                            0 
                         
                      
                    so that  
                      f 
                    restricted to that neighborhood is one-to-one (cf.   Bamber & van Santen, 1985, 2000 ). There are results showing that the BLIMs induced by knowledge structures of a particular type exhibit non-identifiability ( Spoto, Stefanutti, & Vidotto, 2012, 2013 ). The following goes beyond these results by providing a complete account of the parameter trade-offs that occur in these as well as other cases, which have not been covered yet.

Doignon and Falmagne (1985)  define a  knowledge structure  as a pair  
                         
                            〈 
                            Q 
                            , 
                            K 
                            〉 
                         
                       in which  
                         Q 
                       is a nonempty set (assumed to be finite throughout the paper), and  
                         K 
                       is a family of subsets of  
                         Q 
                      , containing at least  
                         Q 
                       and the empty set  
                         0̸ 
                      . The set  
                         Q 
                       is called the  domain  of the knowledge structure. Its elements are referred to as  items  and the subsets in the family  
                         K 
                       are labeled  (knowledge) states . A knowledge state represents the subset of items in the considered domain that an individual masters.

In practical applications, we may not assume that a person solves a problem if and only if the person masters this problem (i.e. it is an element of the person’s knowledge state). There are two kinds of errors that can occur. In case of a careless error the person actually masters an items, but does not solve it, whereas solving an item without actually mastering it is called a lucky guess. Handling these types of errors calls for a probabilistic framework. Within such an approach we can also take into account that knowledge states will not occur with equal probability.

The introduction of a probabilistic framework is based on dissociating the knowledge state  
                         K 
                       of a person from the actual given response pattern  
                         R 
                      . Let

                         Q

                      . In the sequel, consideration will be confined to the class of models for which  (1) ,  (2) and (3)  hold, and which are called  basic local independence models  (BLIM).

It is obvious from the above definitions that a BLIM can be considered a latent class model ( Andersen, 1982; Dayton, 1998; Goodman, 1978; Lazarsfeld & Henry, 1968; Vermunt & Magidson, 2004 ) with the knowledge states forming the latent classes. In fact, with  (2) and (3)  holding, a restricted latent class model is obtained ( Schrepp, 2005; Ünlü, 2011 ). Notice that in contrast to general latent class analysis, the knowledge states impose a structure on the set of latent classes which puts constraints on the conditional probabilities of the response patterns given the latent classes. Within the limited scope of linearly ordered latent classes this idea has been implemented in some particular models ( Dayton & Macready, 1976; Lazarsfeld & Henry, 1968; Proctor, 1970 ) that were intended to provide probabilistic versions of Guttman scaling ( Guttman, 1950 ). For details see  Ünlü (2011) .

Various approaches to estimating the parameters of a BLIM have been suggested ( Heller & Wickelmaier, 2012; Schrepp, 1999; Stefanutti & Robusto, 2009 ). All of these contributions do not touch upon the problem of identifiability. There is, however, work showing that there are non-identifiable BLIMs ( Spoto et al., 2012, 2013 ), and software to check given BLIMs (of moderate size) for non-identifiability ( Stefanutti, Heller, Anselmi, & Robusto, 2012 ). While this proves their existence, the following for the first time provides a full theoretical account of the parameter trade-offs in those kind of structures as well as others, which have not been considered before. These cases cover all the instances cropping up in the collection of BLIMs arising from all the possible knowledge structures on a three-item domain. Finally, the paper elaborates on results characterizing important classes of knowledge structures, for which the BLIM built upon them is not identifiable, and provides new results on the possible types of parameter trade-offs that can occur.

Let  
                         Q 
                       be a domain and  
                         K 
                       a knowledge structure on  
                         Q 
                      . Characterizing the BLIM induced by the knowledge structure  
                         K 
                       as a parametric model amounts to specify its parameter and outcome space as well as its prediction function (e.g.,   Bamber & van Santen, 1985 ).

Some of the constraints defining the admissible parameter combinations introduced in this section are quite obvious, while others are more or less tacit assumptions that have not been explicitly addressed in large parts of the literature (but see   Heller & Repitsch, 2012 ).

                            θ 
                            ∈ 
                            Θ 
                         . Its existence is guaranteed because  
                            f 
                          is an analytic function. The importance of the Jacobian matrix  
                            
                               
                                  J 
                               
                               
                                  f 
                               
                            
                          lies in the fact that it represents a linear approximation to  
                            f 
                          near a given point (e.g.,   Bamber & van Santen, 1985 ).

For the present purpose the critical feature of the Jacobian matrix is its  rank , which is identified with the number of linearly independent columns (or, equivalently, rows) of the  
                            m 
                            × 
                            n 
                          matrix, and will be denoted by  
                            rk 
                            
                               ( 
                               
                                  
                                     J 
                                  
                                  
                                     f 
                                  
                               
                               ) 
                            
                         . The rank of  
                            
                               
                                  J 
                               
                               
                                  f 
                               
                            
                          cannot be greater than  
                            m 
                          or  
                            n 
                         . If  
                            rk 
                            
                               ( 
                               
                                  
                                     J 
                                  
                                  
                                     f 
                                  
                               
                               ) 
                            
                            = 
                            min 
                            
                               { 
                               m 
                               , 
                               n 
                               } 
                            
                          then the Jacobian matrix  
                            
                               
                                  J 
                               
                               
                                  f 
                               
                            
                          is said to have  full rank , otherwise it is  rank deficient .

The inequality  
                            m 
                            ≥ 
                            n 
                          is a necessary condition for identifiability of the BLIM. It assures that the number of free parameters  
                            n 
                          does not exceed the number of independent observations  
                            m 
                         , implying the restriction  
                            
                               
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  
                                     | 
                                     Q 
                                     | 
                                  
                               
                            
                            ≥ 
                            2 
                            ⋅ 
                            
                               | 
                               Q 
                               | 
                            
                            + 
                            
                               | 
                               K 
                               | 
                            
                         . Because  
                            
                               | 
                               K 
                               | 
                            
                            ≥ 
                            2 
                          by definition, the inequality cannot be satisfied for  
                            
                               | 
                               Q 
                               | 
                            
                            < 
                            3 
                          by any fully parameterized BLIM. The subsequent section provides a complete overview for  
                            
                               | 
                               Q 
                               | 
                            
                            = 
                            3 
                         , also under certain parameter restrictions.

For the following let us assume that  
                            m 
                            ≥ 
                            n 
                          holds. Then, knowing the rank of the Jacobian matrix of a BLIM allows for drawing conclusions on its (non-)identifiability. It can be shown that if the Jacobian matrix has full rank at some point  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                            ∈ 
                            Θ 
                          then the BLIM is locally identifiable at that point (e.g.,   Smith, 1998 , Appendix A). The converse implication need not be true in general, but it holds for regular points ( Wald, 1950 ). A point  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                            ∈ 
                            Θ 
                          is called  regular  whenever the rank of the Jacobian matrix is constant in an open neighborhood of  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         . For a Jacobian matrix of an analytic function the set of irregular points is of Lebesgue measure zero, and thus almost all points are regular. This follows, for example, from  Fisher (1966, Theorem 5.A.2) .  Bamber and van Santen (1985, Proposition 20)  show that, under some fairly weak conditions holding in the present case, whenever the Jacobian matrix is rank deficient everywhere in  
                            Θ 
                          then the BLIM is not identifiable. To be precise, under the assumptions of  
                            f 
                          being continuously differentiable and of  
                            Θ 
                          being an open subset of  
                            
                               
                                  R 
                               
                               
                                  n 
                               
                            
                          observing that  
                            rk 
                            
                               ( 
                               
                                  
                                     J 
                                  
                                  
                                     f 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  θ 
                                  ) 
                               
                               ) 
                            
                            < 
                            n 
                          for all  
                            θ 
                            ∈ 
                            Θ 
                          leads to this conclusion.

Determining the rank of the Jacobian matrix in an algebraic way (as will be done subsequently in Section   3 ) provides the maximum rank that is attained at some point(s) in the parameter space. If the rank of the Jacobian matrix can only be determined numerically at particular points in the parameter space then a priori we cannot be sure to have identified its maximum rank. In this situation, however, a result by  Bamber and van Santen (1985)  assures that, if the prediction function is analytic then the Jacobian matrix has maximum rank almost everywhere in  
                            Θ 
                         . This opens up an avenue to numerically computing the maximum rank of the Jacobian matrix. Randomly selecting several points from the parameter space (e.g., with respect to a uniform distribution on  
                            Θ 
                         ) and evaluating the Jacobian matrix at these points one can be almost sure to detect its maximum rank.

In order to get some overview over (local) identifiability in BLIMs, we consider all possible knowledge structures on the three-item knowledge domain  
                         Q 
                         = 
                         
                            { 
                            a 
                            , 
                            b 
                            , 
                            c 
                            } 
                         
                      .  Fig. 1 
                       shows the Hasse diagrams of representatives of the equivalence classes of knowledge structures on  
                         Q 
                       that are identical up to relabeling. As the prediction functions and the Jacobian matrices associated to the knowledge structures within each of the classes are also identical up to relabeling, it suffices to check identifiability for only one representative of each of the 20 equivalence classes  
                         
                            
                               K 
                            
                            
                               00 
                            
                         
                       to  
                         
                            
                               K 
                            
                            
                               19 
                            
                         
                       listed in  Fig. 1 . Notice that knowledge structures that are identical up to relabeling are isomorphic knowledge structures, i.e. there is a bijection from one domain onto the other such that the image of any knowledge state is a knowledge state ( Doignon & Falmagne, 1999 ). Notice also that knowledge structures isomorphic as lattices need not be isomorphic in the just defined sense (consider  
                         
                            
                               K 
                            
                            
                               01 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               K 
                            
                            
                               02 
                            
                         
                      , or  
                         
                            
                               K 
                            
                            
                               04 
                            
                         
                      ,  
                         
                            
                               K 
                            
                            
                               05 
                            
                         
                      ,  
                         
                            
                               K 
                            
                            
                               06 
                            
                         
                      , for example).

The interested reader is referred to the  Appendix  for more details on this classification. It presents combinatorial as well as group-theoretic results on the number and size of the equivalence classes which are not essential for the subsequent development, but complement it.

Given the prediction function of a knowledge structure, Mathematica ( Wolfram Research, 2010 ) was used to compute the Jacobian matrix and to determine its rank. Computing the vectors generating the null space (whenever nontrivial) turned out to be an extremely memory intensive task. With the available resources the vectors could not always be returned in a digestible format, with the output filling scores and scores of pages.

If identifiability is not conceived as a property of a probabilistic model, but rather as a property of its parameters, then the above results indicate that in the considered BLIM the parameters  
                         
                            
                               β 
                            
                            
                               c

The following identifies properties of the underlying knowledge structure  
                         K 
                       that cause the associated BLIM to have a rank deficient Jacobian matrix. In doing this, the examples of Section   3  are revisited. It is however important to notice that the subsequently derived results hold for arbitrary BLIMs and are neither limited to domains of particular cardinality, nor to the no-guessing case considered in the examples above.

This section provides various results showing that for certain classes of knowledge structures on a (finite) domain  
                            Q 
                          the columns of the Jacobian matrix  
                            
                               
                                  J 
                               
                               
                                  f 
                               
                            
                          of the associated BLIM are not linearly independent at all points of the parameter space, and  
                            
                               
                                  J 
                               
                               
                                  f 
                               
                            
                          thus is rank deficient everywhere. By  Bamber and van Santen (1985, Proposition 20) , we may then conclude that the BLIMs associated to these knowledge structures are not identifiable.

The knowledge structure  
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  02 
                               
                            
                            = 
                            
                               { 
                               0̸ 
                               , 
                               
                                  { 
                                  a 
                                  , 
                                  b 
                                  } 
                               
                               , 
                               Q 
                               } 
                            
                         , examined closely in Section   3 , is of a particular type. If item  
                            c 
                          is deleted from  
                            Q 
                          (the only knowledge state containing  
                            c 
                         ), then the knowledge state  
                            
                               { 
                               a 
                               , 
                               b 
                               } 
                            
                          results. In general, consider the situation where there is some item  
                            q 
                            ∈ 
                            Q 
                          such that for each state  
                            K 
                          in  
                            K 
                          the subset  
                            K 
                            ∖ 
                            
                               { 
                               q 
                               } 
                            
                          is also a state in  
                            K 
                         . Adopting a notion coined by  Spoto (2011) , a knowledge structure  
                            K 
                          for which this property holds is called  backward-graded in  
                         
                            q 
                          (see also  Spoto et al., 2012 ). With the definition

                                        q

                                           K 
                                        
                                        
                                           q 
                                        
                                     
                                     } 
                                  
                               
                            
                         
                         
                            K 
                          is backward-graded in  
                            q 
                          whenever  
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  − 
                                  q 
                               
                            
                            ⊆ 
                            K 
                         .  Proposition 1  is related to results by  Spoto et al. (2012, 2013) . However, instead of specifying a particular relationship between two points in the parameter space that leaves the model’s prediction invariant, it provides a complete account of the dependence relation among the columns of the Jacobian matrix. This fully characterizes the occurring parameter trade-offs, as exemplified in the previous section.

The following results contribute to providing an answer to the question whether non-identifiable BLIMs are likely to be encountered in practical applications. They consider relevant classes of knowledge structures (on finite domains) that satisfy additional structural properties, and characterize necessary and sufficient conditions for forward- and backward-gradedness.  Spoto et al. (2013)  obtained similar results. They identified conditions under which knowledge spaces, quasi ordinal and ordinal knowledge spaces, or well-graded knowledge spaces (also called learning spaces;  Falmagne & Doignon, 2011 ) are forward- or backward-graded in an item. This section strengthens these results by providing necessary and sufficient conditions, and extends them by deriving characterizations from a different point of view (i.e., from an order-theoretic perspective).

We refer to standard terminology as introduced in  Doignon and Falmagne (1999) . A knowledge structure that is closed for union is called a  knowledge space , while a knowledge structure that closed for both union and intersection is called a  quasi ordinal  knowledge space. The latter notion is due to the fact that there is a one-to-one correspondence between the collection of all knowledge structures  
                         K 
                       on a domain  
                         Q 
                       that are closed for both union and intersection and the quasi orders  
                         ≼ 
                       (i.e., reflexive and transitive binary relations) on  
                         Q 
                       ( Birkhoff, 1937 ), sometimes called  precedence relations .

Concerning knowledge spaces, the subsequent  Proposition 4  is equivalent to results by  Spoto et al. (2013, Corollaries 3 and 7) . Its proof, referring to the atoms of the knowledge space, however, is more direct and shorter than theirs, which is based on the correspondence to surmise functions. A state  
                         B 
                       is an  atom  at some item  
                         q 
                         ∈ 
                         Q 
                       if  
                         B 
                       is minimal (again, with respect to set inclusion) among those states in  
                         K 
                       containing  
                         q 
                      . The conditions stated are necessary and sufficient for a knowledge space being forward- or backward-graded in an item.

Now, the following result characterizes quasi ordinal knowledge spaces that are forward-, or backward-graded through necessary and sufficient properties of the corresponding precedence relation. This quite natural characterization is new.  
                         Corollary 1 
                         
                            Let  
                            
                               K 
                             
                            be a quasi ordinal knowledge space on the domain  
                            
                               Q 
                             
                            and  
                            
                               ≼ 
                            
                            the corresponding precedence relation on  
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            . Then, for  
                            
                               q 
                               ∈ 
                               Q

                                        K 
                                      
                                     is forward-graded in  
                                     
                                        q 
                                      
                                     iff for all  
                                     
                                        p 
                                        ∈ 
                                        Q 
                                     
                                     :  
                                     
                                        p 
                                        ≼ 
                                        q 
                                      
                                     implies  
                                     
                                        p 
                                        = 
                                        q 
                                      
                                     (i.e.,  
                                     
                                        q 
                                      
                                     is a unique minimal element). 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ii. 
                                  
                                     
                                        K 
                                      
                                     is backward-graded in  
                                     
                                        q 
                                      
                                     iff for all

                                        Q

                                        p 
                                      
                                     implies  
                                     
                                        p 
                                        = 
                                        q 
                                      
                                     (i.e.,  
                                     
                                        q 
                                      
                                     is a unique maximal element);

Notice that for

                         Q

                         Q 
                       maximal and minimal elements exist. The quasi ordinal knowledge spaces corresponding to partial orders on  
                         Q

                         Corollary 2 
                         
                            The BLIM induced by an ordinal knowledge space is not identifiable.

The BLIM induced by an ordinal knowledge space is not identifiable.

It may be remarked that this result also follows from noticing that each ordinal knowledge space is well-graded, and well-graded knowledge spaces can be shown to be forward-graded in some item ( Spoto et al., 2013 , Corollary 4).

Section   3  exemplifies that the linear dependence relations derived in  Propositions 1–3  characterize the trade-offs between parameters that leave the predicted probability of the response patterns invariant. These results give rise to conjectures about the kind of trade-offs that lead to non-identifiability of a BLIM. These kinds of trade-offs do not only arise in connection with forward- and backward-gradedness. This fact is demonstrated by the subsequent result, which also casts new light on the role of equally informative items with respect to identifiability. Given a knowledge structure  
                         
                            〈 
                            Q 
                            , 
                            K 
                            〉 
                         
                       two items  
                         p 
                         , 
                         q 
                         ∈ 
                         Q 
                       are considered to be  equally informative  whenever they co-occur in exactly the same knowledge states, i.e.   
                         
                            
                               K

                         = 
                         
                            
                               K 
                            
                            
                               q

                         a

                         ∈ 
                         Q 
                         = 
                         
                            {

                            , 
                            b 
                            ,

                       and  
                         
                            
                               K 
                            
                            
                               04 
                            
                         
                       (see  Fig. 1 ). On the other hand, discriminative knowledge structures, like the ordinal or well-graded knowledge spaces considered above, do not contain any equally informative items.

For equally informative items  
                         p 
                         , 
                         q 
                         ∈ 
                         Q 
                       it is immediate from the definitions that the knowledge structure is neither backward-graded, nor forward-graded in both of them. Since equally informative items do not satisfy the property assumed in  Proposition 2 , too, they meet none of the above considered conditions that imply non-identifiability of the corresponding BLIM. This is why  Spoto et al. (2013)  propose a construction that introduces an equally informative item for each item in  
                         Q 
                       as a remedy in case of non-identifiability. They “conjecture that the BLIM based on the resulting knowledge structure is always identifiable” and claim that this “conjecture is proven to be true for knowledge structures on small sets of items” ( Spoto et al., 2013 , p. 249, Abstract).  Proposition 6  proves that this claim, even if confined to local identifiability, is wrong. It shows that the columns of the Jacobian matrix related to the parameters

The problem with the conjecture of  Spoto et al. (2013)  arises from the fact that they do not give a formal proof, but rather collect evidence from simulation studies. While the parameter space  
                         Θ 
                       itself has positive Lebesgue measure in  
                         
                            
                               R 
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , the subsets of the parameter space defined by the equality constraints in  Propositions 5 and 6 , respectively, have Lebesgue measure zero. Randomly selecting from  
                         Θ 
                      , as in the simulation studies, a point from these subsets will be drawn with probability zero. This is presumably why  Spoto et al. (2013)  overgeneralized their results, which at best only allow for the conclusion that the Jacobian matrix has full rank almost everywhere in  
                         Θ 
                       ( Bamber & van Santen, 1985 ).

The results in this section have shown that there are subsets of the parameter space of Lebesgue measure zero, where we have direct trade-offs between  
                         β 
                      - and  
                         η 
                      -parameters. This leaves the possibility open that none of these trade-offs occur almost everywhere in  
                         Θ 
                      . Consequently, the above mentioned conjectures have to be restated properly as follows. Formal proofs of these conjectures are still missing.  
                         Conjecture 1 
                         
                            The subset of the parameter space with trade-offs between parameters due to non-identifiability of a BLIM not involving any of the  
                            
                               π 
                            
                            -parameters has Lebesgue measure zero.

The subset of the parameter space with trade-offs between parameters due to non-identifiability of a BLIM not involving any of the  
                            
                               π 
                            
                            -parameters has Lebesgue measure zero.

Conjecture 2 
                         
                            The subset of the parameter space with equally informative items involved in trade-offs between parameters due to non-identifiability of a BLIM has Lebesgue measure zero.

The subset of the parameter space with equally informative items involved in trade-offs between parameters due to non-identifiability of a BLIM has Lebesgue measure zero.

Considering the so-called basic local independence model (BLIM;   Doignon & Falmagne, 1999 ), the present paper addresses the problem of its local identifiability. Based on a formal characterization of parametric models ( Bamber & van Santen, 1985, 2000 ), it extends previous results by providing a full account of the occurring parameter trade-offs. An overview of the BLIMs that arise from the possible knowledge structures on a three-item domain (see  Fig. 1 ) not only supplies illustrative examples of non-identifiable models, but forms the starting point for generalizing these particular cases. The theoretical results derived in Section   4  hold for arbitrary BLIMs and are neither limited to domains of particular cardinality, nor to the no-guessing case considered in Section   3 . Section   5  shows that non-identifiability is not a rarely encountered event. On the contrary, the BLIMs defined on the types of knowledge structures which have received most attention turn out to be non-identifiable. Section   6  further characterizes the kinds of parameter trade-offs that occur in case of non-identifiability. It demonstrates that properties that were conjectured to hold for all points in the parameter space, may only hold almost everywhere.

It has to be emphasized that the presented approach provides a characterization of local identifiability of the BLIM, i.e., identifiability in the vicinity of a point. This allows for drawing some conclusions about global non-identifiability, but in general not about global identifiability. Even if the model is locally identifiable everywhere, then it need not be globally identifiable. However, whenever the Jacobian matrix is rank deficient at every point in the parameter space, then the BLIM is globally non-identifiable ( Bamber & van Santen, 1985 , Proposition 20). This is exactly what can be concluded from  Propositions 1  to  3 .

Non-identifiability causes serious problems to knowledge assessment, which tries to single out a person’s knowledge state. Using a Bayesian rule, assessment is commonly based on the posterior distribution on the knowledge states given the observed response pattern. The assessed state is identified with the maximum a posteriori (MAP) estimate. If state probabilities are not determined uniquely (as is the case in all of the non-identifiable BLIMs treated above) then the MAP estimate may not be determined uniquely, too. Take as an example the already discussed BLIM defined on the knowledge structure

While the presented results can account for each of the instances of non-identifiability listed in  Table 1 , this does not mean that it covers all conditions causing non-identifiability. In larger domains, there may crop up cases other than those treated here. However, it becomes increasingly difficult to identify them. A systematic treatment of all possible BLIMs gets out of reach very quickly as the number of items in the domain increases (see  Appendix ). Moreover, the symbolic calculation of the rank of the Jacobian matrix of a BLIM with the help of computer algebra systems, providing results that are valid for all points in the parameter space, becomes more and more time consuming and memory intensive.  Stefanutti et al. (2012)  implemented a routine for assessing local identifiability of a given BLIM (up to moderate size) by numerically calculating the rank of the Jacobian matrix of its prediction function at a point in the parameter space and for tracking down the non-unique parameters. Proceeding in this way, however, it is difficult, if not impossible, to decide whether the obtained results hold for every point of the parameter space, or only almost everywhere. Clarifying issues like that remains a theoretical challenge. Some preliminary results have been presented, but a number of questions is left open.

I am grateful to two anonymous reviewers for their valuable comments on previous versions, and in particular to Jean-Paul Doignon for his thoughtful suggestions that he made as acting editor of the manuscript. Thanks go also to Thomas M. Keller for pointing me to relevant papers on the symmetric group operating on families of subsets.

The following provides a characterization of the equivalence classes of isomorphic knowledge structures on a domain  
                         Q 
                      . It draws upon combinatorial as well as group-theoretic results.

The number of equivalence classes for a given domain  
                         Q 
                       of  
                         n 
                       items can be derived from well-known integer sequences. In fact, the considered equivalence classes are in one-to-one correspondence to the improper covers of an unlabeled  
                         n 
                      -set. A  cover  of a set  
                         X 
                       is a collection of nonempty subsets of  
                         X 
                       whose union contains  
                         X 
                      . The cover is said to be  proper  if  
                         X 
                       is not a member of the collection, and  improper  otherwise. Notice that a knowledge structure on  
                         X 
                       results if we add the empty set to any improper cover of  
                         X 
                      . Because the equivalence classes collect knowledge structures that are identical up to relabeling, the set  
                         X 
                       is considered to be unlabeled. Taking the difference of the integer sequences A055621 and A055152 of covers and proper covers of an unlabeled  
                         n 
                      -set ( Sloane, n.d. ) then allows to compute the number of the considered equivalence classes on a domain consisting of  
                         n 
                       items. The number of equivalence classes for  
                         k 
                         = 
                         4 
                         , 
                         5 
                         , 
                         6 
                       is 996,  
                         9 
                         
                         333 
                         
                         312 
                      , and 6 406 603 084 568 576, respectively.

For deriving results on the size of the equivalence classes, we recall some basic concepts. For a more comprehensive introduction of these notions consult  Rotman (1995) , or any other standard textbook on group theory.
